        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202406547')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="401b571a830f47dda946ba3491248d06"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="401b571a830f47dda946ba3491248d06">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'defe8e71-75f5-4b4c-80d7-1ea35694f131'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '8722AD1F7EED87328E58DA51A186D472';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d62115263" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Transition metal sulfides are recognized as an excellent alternative to sodium ion anodes ascribed to the outstanding theoretical capacity. The unique crystal arrangement of VS<sub>4</sub> gives it exceptional theoretical capacity, despite challenges like insufficient electrical conductivity and undesirable volume expansion. Herein, a novel stabilized anode featuring a distinctive 3D hollow spherical structure is proposed, providing a simple strategy to synthesize such anodes for VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs bonded via C-O-S and V-O-C interfaces. The kinetic investigations and density functional theory reveal that the unique structure connected by interfacial bonds enhances Na<sup>+</sup> transport rate and charge transfer efficiency, while carbon greatly mitigates the volume expansion. Unsurprisingly, the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs exhibit an impressive first-cycle Coulombic efficiency of 91.31% and an ultrahigh reversible capacity of 612 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, even exhibit the reversible capacity of 498.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Additionally, the NaFePO<sub>4</sub>//VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs full cell is cycled for 200 cycles at 0.2 C and powered the light-emitting diodes for up to 30 minutes afterward. Overall, this work enhances the conductivity and stability of the material by combining VS<sub>4</sub> with hollow carbon mesoporous spheres through interfacial bonding, offering an efficient strategy to anode materials in sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202406547-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202406547-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The ongoing depletion of hydrocarbon reserves has spurred the advancement of alternative renewable energy sources as well as the innovation of power conversion and storage technologies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0001_R_d62115255e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - SusMat">1</a>-<a href="#smll202406547-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0005_R_d62115255e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ACS Energy Lett.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs)&nbsp;are widely employed owing to high energy density, but the development has been hindered by limited resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0006_R_d62115255e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Small Struct.">6</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0007_R_d62115255e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Electrochim. Acta">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fortunately, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) share similar energy storage mechanism to LIBs, and the abundance of natural sodium resources contributes to the economic benefits in large-scale application, rendering SIBs a feasible alternative to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0008_R_d62115255e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">8</a>-<a href="#smll202406547-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0015_R_d62115255e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Chem. A">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, a larger ionic radius (1.02 Å) of Na<sup>+</sup> than Li<sup>+</sup> restricts kinetic diffusion and induces obvious volume expansion, ultimately compromising the durability of the electrode configuration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0016_R_d62115255e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Small Struct.">16</a>-<a href="#smll202406547-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0018_R_d62115255e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Mater. Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, developing anode materials with rapid kinetic properties and robust structural stability through strategic design is crucial for advancing SIB development.</p>
         
         <p>Transition metal sulfides (TMSs) with weak Van der Waals forces between layers have been investigated, and it has been found that increasing the desired layer spacing facilitates the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> and promotes the performance in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0019_R_d62115255e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Energy Storage">19</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0020_R_d62115255e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Coord. Chem. Rev.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> TMSs particularly vanadium sulfides (VS<i><sub>x</sub></i>), such as V<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, V<sub>5</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, VS<sub>2</sub>, V<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, and VS<sub>4</sub>, possess various crystal structures that provide appropriate interlayer spacing for facilitating the accommodation and transfer of Na<sup>+</sup>, thus showcasing better overall performance in SIBs. Apart from providing sufficient interlayer spacing for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction, VS<i><sub>x</sub></i> exhibits multiple variable valence states of V, which contribute to storing multiple S ions and thereby enhance sodium storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0021_R_d62115255e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0022_R_d62115255e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The special crystal structure and high theoretical capacity of VS<sub>4</sub> have attracted the attention of researchers. There are repeating units like 1D atomic chain structure of V<sup>4+</sup>(S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup>)<sub>2</sub> with a chain spacing of 5.83 Å in the structure of VS<sub>4</sub>, which significantly surpasses the ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> and provides sufficient space for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. Moreover, this arrangement supports efficient Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0023_R_d62115255e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Rec.">23</a>-<a href="#smll202406547-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0027_R_d62115255e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The multiple valence states of V from V<sup>5+</sup> to V<sup>0</sup> and high proportion of sulfur content allow VS<sub>4</sub> molecule to accommodate more Na<sup>+</sup> such that the theoretical capacity of VS<sub>4</sub> is as high as 1197 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0028_R_d62115255e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano-Micro Lett.">28</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0029_R_d62115255e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Insufficient conductivity and volume expansion are major factors to hinder the advancement of VS<sub>4</sub>. These limitations significantly decrease battery stability during high current densities and prolonged cycling test, greatly impacting their practical applicability. Researchers have proposed various methods to figure out these issues, primarily focusing on modifying material structure, compositing carbon material, and exploring electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0023_R_d62115255e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Rec.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Yang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0029_R_d62115255e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> proposed the synthesis of highly crystalline sea urchin-like VS<sub>4</sub> to improve electronic transport efficiency and provide a large surface area to accommodate volume changes. Furthermore, hollow microsphere VS<sub>4</sub> can effectively mitigate volume change that occurs during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction, maintaining a reversible capacity of 378 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0030_R_d62115255e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Small">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the structural modification can alleviate the volume expansion to some extent, the insufficient conductivity still remains for the materials. To solve this dilemma, a modification strategy for compositing with carbon materials is proposed, including VS<sub>4</sub>/CNTs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0031_R_d62115255e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">31</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0032_R_d62115255e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0033_R_d62115255e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">33</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0034_R_d62115255e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Mater. Chem. A">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> PANI-VS<sub>4</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0035_R_d62115255e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> VS<sub>4</sub>@L-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0036_R_d62115255e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Power Sources">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Carbon materials serve not only to effectively address problem of the poor electrical conductivity but also to reduce the inherent volume expansion of the material. However, many reports propose strategies for compositing with carbon materials without elucidating the interaction between VS<sub>4</sub> and carbon materials.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we present a strategy to attach VS<sub>4</sub> onto the surface of hollow carbon mesoporous spheres (VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs) through S−O−C and V−O−C interfacial bonds. The hollow carbon mesoporous spheres as skeleton support significantly reduces the volume expansion brought about by the insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby improving the cycling stability of the VS<sub>4</sub> material. Moreover, the surface bonding enhances electronic conductivity and sodium adsorption, ensuring excellent rate performance over multiple cycles. After 510 cycles, the material retains a capacity of 458 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and it maintains a capacity of 489.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles. Meanwhile, the double S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup> provides excellent sodium storage capacity for this composite (612 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202406547-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202406547-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs were manufactured using a one-step solvothermal method, employing hollow carbon mesoporous spheres prepared by the hard-templating approach as a skeleton for the VS<sub>4</sub>. The synthesis route of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs is shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0001">1a</a>. Tetraethyl orthosilicate, resorcinol, and formaldehyde are used to coat resorcinol-formaldehyde (RF) oligomer on the surface of SiO<sub>2</sub> by an acetalization reaction, forming the SiO<sub>2</sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub>/RF yolk-shell structure. Subsequently, carbonation reaction occurred under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere from SiO<sub>2</sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub>/RF to SiO<sub>2</sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub>/C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). SiO<sub>2</sub> is removed by acid leaching treatment to prepare hollow carbon mesoporous spheres (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), and then thioacetamide and sodium metavanadate are used as S and V sources to grow on the interface of hollow carbon mesoporous spheres by the solvothermal method.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406547-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/06f22a7e-50b4-4a1d-be8a-5f07607f4e72/smll202406547-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/06f22a7e-50b4-4a1d-be8a-5f07607f4e72/smll202406547-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0f1c0b1a-6013-42d9-a190-467ec7a17706/smll202406547-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/06f22a7e-50b4-4a1d-be8a-5f07607f4e72/smll202406547-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406547-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406547" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram of the fabrication. b) XRD pattern of VS<sub>4</sub>, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-4, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8, and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-12. c) XRD pattern of 2<i>θ</i> in the range of 14–20°. d,e) Raman spectra of VS<sub>4</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 in different wavelength bands.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) patterns of pure phase VS<sub>4</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs with different carbon composite ratios (4, 8, 12 wt.%) are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0001">1b,c</a>. Importantly, the major characteristic peaks of all samples in XRD spectra align well with monoclinic VS<sub>4</sub> (PDF#72-1294). The absence of additional peaks in XRD patterns of all the samples demonstrates that the amount of HCMSs did not affect the generation of VS<sub>4</sub> (VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-<i>x</i> (g), <i>x</i> denotes the amount of HCMSs). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0001">1c</a>, the observed diffraction peak in the (110) plane of VS<sub>4</sub> shifts to a lower angle as the HCMSs content increases. According to Bragg's equation: 2<i>d</i>·sin<i>θ</i> = <i>λ</i>, the spacing between the VS<sub>4</sub> layers may increase as a result of the addition of carbon material during solvothermal synthesis. As the diffraction peaks of carbon disappear in the XRD spectra, more structural and elemental information about the complexes were evaluated by Raman spectra. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0001">1d</a> reveals the presence of chemical bonding vibrations of VS<sub>4</sub> in the sample in which the double peak at 551 cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the telescoping vibration of the S−S bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0026_R_d62115255e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0033_R_d62115255e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">33</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0037_R_d62115255e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 191 and 218 cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to the stretching modes of V−S bonds, while the peak at 270 cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the bending mode of V−S bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0038_R_d62115255e726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Eng. J.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fluctuations at 347 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 551 cm<sup>−1</sup> are ascribed to the respiration mode of the V<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> cage and the stretching mode of the S−S bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0039_R_d62115255e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">39</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0040_R_d62115255e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Chem. Eng. J.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0001">1e</a>, the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs exhibit D and G bands that closely resemble those of HCMSs, indicating successful complexation of VS<sub>4</sub> with HCMSs. In addition, the two weak peaks located at 817 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 910 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be attributed to the V−O−C bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0041_R_d62115255e758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the characteristic D band (at 1335 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and G band (at 1590 cm<sup>−1</sup>) are associated with defects in the sp<sup>3</sup> carbon atoms and the ordered structure of the sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms in the plane, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0042_R_d62115255e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Power Sources">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Apparently, the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs (0.94) is higher than that of pure VS<sub>4</sub> (0.82). It shows that V−O−C is connected to carbon defects on the surface of hollow carbon mesoporous spheres.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0043_R_d62115255e786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Conformational characterization of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs and precursors was conducted by field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) and high resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM). The pure VS<sub>4</sub> exhibits agglomerated large-sized hollow flower spheres (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), which hinder the resolution of the volume expansion problem that occurs when Na<sup>+</sup> ions are dislodged/embedded, and result in small effective areas of contact amongst the material and the electrolyte. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0002">2a</a> illustrates the microscopic morphology of hollow carbon mesoporous microspheres, revealing homogeneous hollow spheres prepared via the hard template method. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0002">2b</a>, the morphology of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs which resembles sesame seed balls reveals a 3D hollow spherical structure measuring ≈200–300&nbsp;nm in diameter. The hollow spherical structure of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs is clearly depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0002">2c,d</a>. Therefore, the size of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs is much smaller than that of pure VS<sub>4</sub> with diameters ≈2.5&nbsp;µm. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), it can be shown that VS<sub>4</sub> is uniformly distributed on the surface of the hollow carbon mesoporous spheres. The hollow carbon mesoporous spheres provide the composites with more impressive electrical permeability and stability, conducive to stable transmission of Na<sup>+</sup> and electrons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0044_R_d62115255e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Energy Mater.">44</a>-<a href="#smll202406547-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0047_R_d62115255e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - RSC Adv.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The specific surface area and porosity of pure VS<sub>4</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs with different carbon composite ratios were further investigated using nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherms (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The inclusion of HCMSs greatly improved the specific surface area and porosity of materials upon reincorporation (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Although the elevated specific surface area resulted from the addition of HCMSs, the inappropriate addition caused disruption of the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs complexation mechanism. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-4 forms a pure VS<sub>4</sub> morphology owing to the under-addition of HCMSs, while most of the carbon spheres uncovered with VS<sub>4</sub> due to partial over-addition of HCMSs. In addition, the growth of VS<sub>4</sub> on HCMSs greatly augments the specific surface area, diminishes the degree of agglomeration, improves the effective area between material and electrolyte, and shortens the distance that electrons need to travel for transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0048_R_d62115255e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Carbon">48</a>-<a href="#smll202406547-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0050_R_d62115255e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Energy Storage">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0002">2e,f</a> presents the HRTEM image of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8, reveals an interlayer distance of 0.562 and 0.579&nbsp;nm which matches to the (110) plane of VS<sub>4</sub>. Additionally, the spacing of the layers is slightly greater than VS<sub>4</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), which aligns with the findings from the XRD analysis, suggesting that the interplanar spacing of VS<sub>4</sub> becomes wider after complexing with HCMSs, making it more conducive to sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0038_R_d62115255e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Eng. J.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern confirmed the presence of diffraction rings for (114), (110), and (020) planes, thus confirming that VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 is polycrystalline nature like pure VS<sub>4</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0002">2g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). High-angle annular dark-field STEM (HAADF-STEM) images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0002">2h</a>) and elemental mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0002">2i–l</a>) confirm the uniform distribution of ≈100&nbsp;nm VS<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles on the surface of hollow carbon spheres, highlighting their attachment to the surface of sphere. The significant reduction in particle sizes of as-synthesized VS<sub>4</sub> may stem from the abnormal growth environment facilitated by HCMSs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406547-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/801b5c6a-cd0d-47de-a3bf-8ead4182cd6e/smll202406547-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/801b5c6a-cd0d-47de-a3bf-8ead4182cd6e/smll202406547-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e2ea223c-6173-4aac-a001-f043086a0007/smll202406547-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/801b5c6a-cd0d-47de-a3bf-8ead4182cd6e/smll202406547-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406547-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406547" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) FESEM image of hollow carbon mesoporous spheres; b) FESEM image, c,d) TEM images, e,f) HRTEM images, g) SAED image, h) HAADF-STEM image, and i–l) elemental mapping analysis of V, S, C, and all elements overlap of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR, <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0003">3a</a>) shows the absorption peaks of S−V−S (551 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and V = S (991 cm<sup>−1</sup>) in both samples of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0051_R_d62115255e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Electrochim. Acta">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The weak peak appears at ≈883 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be attributed to the C−O−S interface bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0052_R_d62115255e956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Alloys Compd.">52</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0053_R_d62115255e956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nanoscale">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the appearance of broad peaks at 1164 and 1600 cm<sup>−1</sup> for VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs demonstrates the presence of oxygen-containing functional groups at the surface of the etched porous carbon spheres.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0054_R_d62115255e966" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed to further analyze the chemical valence information of the prepared samples. The XPS spectra of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub> are present in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0003">3b</a>. VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 displayed a relatively sharp peak of C 1s than VS<sub>4</sub>, which suggests that VS<sub>4</sub> is distributed on the surface of HCMSs. The peaks placed at 289.9, 285.9, and 284.8&nbsp;eV are C═O, C−O, and C−C chemical bonds, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0003">3c</a>). In addition, the proportion of peaks ≈286&nbsp;eV in the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 sample is significantly increased, which could be attributed to partial sulfur doping in the carbon structure, leading to the formation of specific S−O−C bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0055_R_d62115255e991" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Adv. Energy Mater.">55</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0052_R_d62115255e991" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Alloys Compd.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 530.4 and 531.7&nbsp;eV indicate the formation of V−O−C and V−O bonds, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0003">3d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0056_R_d62115255e999" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">56</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0057_R_d62115255e999" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Compos. Interfaces">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This suggests that the chemical bond of V−O−C is formed when VS<sub>4</sub> grows on porous carbon, which is not just due to the result of physical adsorption.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0041_R_d62115255e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0058_R_d62115255e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - J. Mater. Chem. A">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0003">3e</a>, the XPS spectra of S 2p of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub> show double peaks of S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> at 164.4 and 163.1&nbsp;eV, respectively, which are related to the S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup> of VS<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0025_R_d62115255e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of a peak at 165.1&nbsp;eV in VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 implies the formation of S−O bonds following the synthesis of the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 composite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0059_R_d62115255e1039" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">59</a>-<a href="#smll202406547-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0061_R_d62115255e1039" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Small">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This formation potentially strengthens the structural stability. The peak at ≈168.8 is assigned to S−O<i><sub>x</sub></i>, attributed to a slight oxidation occurring on the surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0062_R_d62115255e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Nanoscale">62</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0063_R_d62115255e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Energy Chem.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the high-resolution V 2p XPS spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0003">3f</a>), two strong peaks situated near 517.2 and 524.6&nbsp;eV indicate the presence of V<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively. The weak peaks at 514.3 and 521.9&nbsp;eV indicate the presence of V<sup>3+</sup> in VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0064_R_d62115255e1071" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Int. J. Hydrogen Energy">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The appearance of V<sup>3+</sup> may be attributed to the solvothermal method where a reduction reaction occurs, reducing the vanadium ions from the higher valence state to the lower valence state. The XPS peak area of V 2p for V<sup>3+</sup> is less than that of V<sup>4+</sup>, revealing that the compound is mainly dominated by V<sup>4+</sup>. The VS<sub>4</sub> is anchored to the mesoporous carbon microspheres through the formed V−O−C and S−O−C interfacial bonds, which enhances the electrical permeability as well as the structural stability of VS<sub>4</sub>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406547-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6273e48c-5b0b-4c03-b370-f5b9b39c8935/smll202406547-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6273e48c-5b0b-4c03-b370-f5b9b39c8935/smll202406547-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e7e48f9a-e70c-4cc9-8053-3d6437428c02/smll202406547-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6273e48c-5b0b-4c03-b370-f5b9b39c8935/smll202406547-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406547-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406547" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) FTIR and b) XPS spectra of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub>. The XPS spectrum of c) C 1s, d) O 1s, e) S 2p, and f) V 2p.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>By assembling half-cell configurations, a comparative analysis was conducted to assess the electrochemical capability of the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs electrode in relation to that of VS<sub>4</sub>. As displayed in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#smll202406547-fig-0004">4a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), the initial three cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub> electrodes were plotted at scanning rate of 0.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0&nbsp;V to reveal the mechanism of the sodiation/desodiation reactions. The reduced oxidation peaks observed at the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub> electrodes are nearly identical in position, suggesting that the introduction of carbon did not alter the conversion reaction of VS<sub>4</sub>. The reduction peaks in the initial cycle were observed at 1.7&nbsp;V and 0.25&nbsp;V. In the following cycle, two prominent peaks at 0.25 and 1.7&nbsp;V attenuated, which is attributed to the initial embedding of Na<sup>+</sup> in the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs electrode for the conversion reaction and the generation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) membranes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0065_R_d62115255e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Energy Environ. Mater.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the second cycle, the strong peak situated at 1.85&nbsp;V is ascribed to the reduction of VS<sub>4</sub> to Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub>, whereas the peaks at 0.8 and 0.25&nbsp;V imply the further conversion of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>S and V. The oxidation peaks detected at 1.55, 2.1, and 2.32&nbsp;V are indicative of the Na<sub>2</sub>S and V inverse reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0066_R_d62115255e1170" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks exhibited significant overlap during the second and third cycles, indicating good reaction reversibility. The galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0004">4b</a>) of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> show multiple potential plateaus matching to the CV curves, matching Na insertion.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406547-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/da0886f6-ced1-4b66-b814-b541b3b525ff/smll202406547-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/da0886f6-ced1-4b66-b814-b541b3b525ff/smll202406547-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/846080a3-75a4-4973-882e-b0c5f83a5e92/smll202406547-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/da0886f6-ced1-4b66-b814-b541b3b525ff/smll202406547-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406547-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406547" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8; b) GCD curves of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>; c) rate performance of VS<sub>4</sub>, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-4, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8, and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-12; d) cycle performance at current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>; e) cycling performance at high current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> for VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8; f) long cyclic performance of VS<sub>4</sub>, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-4, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8, and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-12 at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0004">4c</a> displays the rate capability of electrodes, delivering average reversible capacities of 640.3, 576.5, 545.5, 523.5, 497.4, 444.2, and 399.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), the initial capacities of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 for discharging and charging were 628.7 and 574.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Additionally, the initial Coulombic efficiency reached a remarkably high value of 91.31%, which is significantly exceptional to that of pure VS<sub>4</sub> (81.58%). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0004">4d</a> describes the plot of long cycle performance for VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> over 300 cycles. It is clear that the capacity keeps dropping from the very beginning to 20 cycles, reaching to 489.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 cycles, and then gradually returns to the normal range after 20 cycles. This drop may result from the activation process of the cell, where the electrodes have a slight decrease in capacity caused by the formation of the SEI membrane.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0067_R_d62115255e1268" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">67</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the reversible capacity of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 remains at 612 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0004">4e</a>, it sustains a high reversible specific capacity of 458 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 510 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Subsequently, to verify the effect of HCMSs on the stability of VS<sub>4</sub> materials, batteries with different amounts of carbon composite were assembled and subjected to long-cycle testing at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The comparison of the long-cycle performance of the composites with different carbon ball incorporations clearly shows that the stability of the VS<sub>4</sub>, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-4 electrode is lower than that of the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8, which can be ascribed to the inappropriate incorporation ratio of carbon material. After 1000 cycles, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 retained a notable reversible specific capacity of 489.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0004">4f</a>). In comparison to previously reported composites, the electrochemical performance of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 demonstrates remarkable superiority (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) and shows exceptional cycling robustness and substantial specific capacity, which can be accredited to the growth of VS<sub>4</sub> on HCMSs via S−O−C and V−O−C interfacial bonds during solvothermal processes. However, compared to VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-4 and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-12, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 manifested superior stability that is associated with the more homogeneous structure of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 with moderate carbon content, which prevents the agglomerated morphological structure of pure VS<sub>4</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The reduction in specific capacity observed in VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 compared to pure VS<sub>4</sub> is attributed to the decreased content of the active substance. However, SEM images taken after 200 cycles at high current densities (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information) show that the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs maintain their basic structure, while the flower-like globular structure of VS<sub>4</sub> has significantly collapsed. This suggests that the addition of carbon material has improved the structural stability of the composite electrode materials. The sesame ball-like VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 has hollow carbon mesoporous spheres as the skeleton, with VS<sub>4</sub> uniformly grown on its surface through S−O−C and C−O−V interfacial bonds, greatly improving the ability to cope with the extreme swelling problem that sodium-ion batteries are prone to appear in the discharging and charging process.</p>
         
         <p>To investigate the ion transport kinetics of the material, we conducted CV profiles at scan rates of 0.2−1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As the sweep rate increases, a similar trend is observed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0005">5a</a> regarding the oxidation and reduction peaks. Subsequently, the equation between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and the scan rate (<i>v</i>) measured by scanning voltammetry (Equations <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) allows us to obtain the <i>b</i> value. The composite exhibits a <i>b</i>-value ranging from 0.76 to 0.99, suggesting the coexistence of two behaviors that collectively contribute to the electrochemical process of battery reaction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). The pseudo-capacitance contribution of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 at all scan speeds gradually increases from 83% to 93% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0005">5b,c</a>). It represents that the high capacity of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 is mainly due to the pseudo-capacitance. The contribution of capacitors tends to reduce compared to pure VS<sub>4</sub>, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-4, and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-12 (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13–S15</a>, Supporting Information) is a result of an increase in the capacitance contribution controlled by diffusion attributed to the special morphology formed by VS<sub>4</sub> growth on HCMSs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0041_R_d62115255e1390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Then, we performed electrochemical impedance spectrometer (EIS) tests. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0005">5d</a>, R<sub>s</sub>, R<sub>ct</sub>, and Z<sub>w</sub> represent intrinsic resistance, charge transfer resistance, and Warburg resistance. The R<sub>ct</sub> value of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 is 30.8 Ω, significantly smaller than that of pure VS<sub>4</sub>. The results indirectly denoted that the charge transfer efficiency of the material is enhanced by compositing with carbon. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0005">5e</a> records the EIS spectra at initial, 3rd, 10th, 50th, and 100th cycles. The charge transfer resistance of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 drops abruptly at the third turn, which is connected to the establishment of a stable SEI layer during sodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0068_R_d62115255e1417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Small">68</a>, <a href="#smll202406547-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0069_R_d62115255e1417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">69</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Throughout the cycling process, the R<sub>ct</sub> of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 remained consistently smaller than that of pristine VS<sub>4</sub>, indicating superior electrochemical properties and stability (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, even after numerous cycles, the charge transfer impedance of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 remained lower than that of VS<sub>4</sub>, suggesting the presence of the enduring ion transport channels within VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0070_R_d62115255e1438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - Small">70</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Then, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was executed to probe the differences in Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion between VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub> (Equations <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0005">5f</a>, this involved a single relaxation process where a current pulse of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> lasts for 10&nbsp;min and then rests for 10&nbsp;min. It can be observed that the D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 is higher than that of VS<sub>4</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0005">5g–i</a>), indicating that the ion transport kinetics of the material were effectively enhanced by compositing porous carbon, thereby improving the electrical permeability of the material.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406547-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fd4aa0fb-5b10-4d4d-b2e9-e147325b8402/smll202406547-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fd4aa0fb-5b10-4d4d-b2e9-e147325b8402/smll202406547-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/50e3ff86-910d-431b-839a-620daaf834f5/smll202406547-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fd4aa0fb-5b10-4d4d-b2e9-e147325b8402/smll202406547-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406547-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406547" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves at different scan rates; b) contribution of capacitance to charge storage at different scan rates; c) proportion of the contribution of capacitive control and diffusion control at different scan rates for VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8; d) Nyquist plots of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub> and an equivalent circuit model; e) Nyquist plots of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 at different cycles; f) signal step of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8; g) GITT curves of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub>; and h,i) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 and VS<sub>4</sub> during the discharging and charging.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To deeply understand the sodium stockpiling mechanism of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8, we assembled half-cells with sodium as the cathode for in situ XRD tests, and the result is presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0006">6a,b</a>. Peaks of VS<sub>4</sub> are visible at 15.9° and 17.1° at the start of the discharge phase, followed by rapid disappearance of peaks when the discharge reaches ≈1.5&nbsp;V. Afterward, the presence of a weak peak at 17.3° is linked to the presence of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub>. The peak of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub> is observed during the initial stage of discharge, possibly related to the disparity in viscosity between Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte and the pole piece, which facilitates rapid penetration of Na<sup>+</sup> into the interior of the electrode. The phenomenon triggers a transformation in part of the VS<sub>4</sub> (VS<sub>4</sub> + <i>x</i>Na<sup>+</sup> + <i>x</i>e<sup>−</sup> → Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub>). The peak intensity of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub> gradually becomes weaker when the discharge approaches 0.8&nbsp;V, and a new peak at 23.7° is attributed to the presence of Na<sub>2</sub>S, representing that a further transformation reaction has taken place (Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub> + (8-<i>x</i>) Na<sup>+</sup> + (8-<i>x</i>) e<sup>−</sup> → 4Na<sub>2</sub>S + V). The peak of Na<sub>2</sub>S is still detected during the beginning of the charging process and a weak peak of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub> appears at 29.4°. This suggests that the transformation from Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>S and V is a reversible process, with some of the Na<sub>2</sub>S undergoing a reversible reaction, potentially contributing to the high specific capacity of the VS<sub>4</sub> material. However, it is significant to mention that during charging to 2&nbsp;V, the Na<sub>2</sub>S peak vanishes while the VS<sub>4</sub> peak is absent (probably in an amorphous form), and the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub> peak emerges until the end of the charging process, implying the sodiation and desodiation processes demonstrate incomplete reversibility during the initial cycle, which might account for the possibility of further improvement in the Coulombic efficiency of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406547-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406547-bib-0030_R_d62115255e1614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Small">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, the surface states of Na and V in the initial and charged/discharged states were investigated using ex situ XPS. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0006">6c</a>, a distinct high-intensity peaks were obtained at 1071&nbsp;eV, revealing the sodification process of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 during the discharge process. The Na fraction decreases after the charging process but still exists, consistent with the result from in situ XRD showing Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub> persists after the charging process. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0006">6d</a>, V is mainly in the form of V<sup>4+</sup> throughout the entire charging and discharging process, and part of V<sup>4+</sup> is reduced to V<sup>3+</sup> or even lower states, leading to an increase in the proportion of V<sup>3+</sup> by the end of the discharging process. This corresponds to the appearance of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub> from Na<sub>2</sub>S when discharged to close to 0.01&nbsp;V. The V<sup>4+</sup>: V<sup>3+</sup> ratio re-increases with the charging process, demonstrating that V<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>3+</sup> undergo reversible conversion reactions during the entire charge and discharge process (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the morphology remained unchanged after undergoing charge/discharge cycles and continued to retain the distinctive structure of VS<sub>4</sub> grown on hollow carbon spheres (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0006">6e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0006">6e,f</a> display the TEM and HRTEM images when first discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, it can be confirmed that Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VS<sub>4</sub> is converted to Na<sub>2</sub>S and V after discharge. The HRTEM image after the charging process reveals the presence of layer spacing with widths of 0.238 and 0.216&nbsp;nm, representing the (440) and (323) crystal planes of Na<sub>3</sub>VS<sub>4</sub>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the uniform distribution of Na and other elements after charge and discharge were observed in HAADF-STEM and elemental dispersive spectral mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0006">6g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The EDS spectra in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information) clearly show a decrease in Na element concentration after the charging state compared to the discharged state, further confirming the effective insertion and extraction of Na in the material. All of these findings align perfectly with the in situ XRD results.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406547-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ab4a78c1-52a9-4953-a616-d2e1f743c39e/smll202406547-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ab4a78c1-52a9-4953-a616-d2e1f743c39e/smll202406547-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/34a47a17-2f0c-4fb8-992f-d0f6d9bb33b5/smll202406547-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ab4a78c1-52a9-4953-a616-d2e1f743c39e/smll202406547-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406547-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406547" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) In situ XRD mapping of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 within a charge/discharge process; X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 at different states of charge and discharge for c) Na 1s and d) V2p; e) TEM and SAED images, f) HRTEM image, g) HAADF-STEM and elemental mapping analyses of V, Na, S, and C of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 after the discharge process.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The density functional theory (DFT) measurements were performed to analyze the reasons for the excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties of the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs anode from a molecular dynamics point of view. The construction of models for pure VS<sub>4</sub>, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs (S−O−C), and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs (V−O−C) is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information). Compared with pure VS4, the electron density near the Fermi energy level after the formation of different interfacial bonds of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs increases according to the density of states (DOS) patterns of pure VS<sub>4</sub>, VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs (S−O−C), and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs (V−O−C) demonstrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0007">7a–c</a>. In addition, the band gap between the valence and conduction bands for the formation of S−O−C (0.1926&nbsp;eV) and V−O−C (0.2&nbsp;eV) interfacial bonds, calculated from the DOS curves, is reduced compared to that of pure VS<sub>4</sub> (0.2378&nbsp;eV). This achievement indicated that the weak physical interaction of interfacial bonds enhanced the electrical conductivity of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0007">7d</a> illustrates the simulated diffusion paths and diffusion energy barriers for sodium on pure VS<sub>4</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs forming different interfacial bonds. It is obvious that the diffusion barrier of sodium ions is significantly reduced in the electrode materials after the formation of interfacial bonds. The model for sodium adsorption by pure VS<sub>4</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs with different interfacial bonds (S−O−C and V−O−C) is described in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0007">7e</a> and Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), with the corresponding adsorption energies for Na are −1.97, −2.24, and −3.67&nbsp;eV, respectively. Evidently, the increased adsorption energy of sodium through interfacial bonds with carbon complexes confers the advantage of improving Na storage capacity. In conclusion, the combination of superior electron conductivity, high sodium adsorption energy, and low sodium diffusion barrier reduction of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs results in excellent multiplicity performance and stability of the electrode materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406547-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a0d992ac-527e-4cea-9c0a-e46260a59403/smll202406547-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a0d992ac-527e-4cea-9c0a-e46260a59403/smll202406547-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f26c1bf7-ab11-4425-a841-6c54045bf849/smll202406547-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a0d992ac-527e-4cea-9c0a-e46260a59403/smll202406547-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406547-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406547" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">DFT calculation simulation analysis. a–c) Density of states (DOS) curves of pure VS<sub>4</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs introducing different interfacial bonds (S−O−C and V−O−C). d) Migration energy barriers of sodium ions in different pathways of pure VS<sub>4</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs (S−O−C and V−O−C). e) Comparison of sodium ion adsorption energies of pure VS<sub>4</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs introducing different interfacial bonds (S−O−C and V−O−C).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To explore the promising application of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8, the full cell was assembled using NaFePO<sub>4</sub> (NFP) as the cathode (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0008">8a</a>). Before assembling the full cell, the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 anode was first pre-sodium by cycling the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 // Na half-cells for three cycles. It can be seen that the electrostatic charge/discharge curves of the full cell exhibit a voltage-coupled plateau, suggesting that the full cell inherits the sodiation and desodiation behaviors of the NFP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0008">8b</a>). The rate performance of NFP // VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 achieves a charging specific capacity of 99.51 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, and an average specific capacity of 55.54, 41.96, 36.27, 32.0, and 27.71 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1 C. Upon the current density returns to 0.1 C, a specific capacity of 59.53 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be recovered (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0008">8c</a>). Through the computation of energy and power densities (Equations <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), the cell can achieve an energy density of 257.1&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at an output power density of 41.77&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>, and still has an excellent performance of 70.84&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at an output power density of 399.12&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406547-fig-0007">7d</a> presents the cyclic performance of the full cell at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, maintaining a capacity of 64.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 67% (the first cycle was chosen for evaluation) after 200 cycles. In addition, the full battery after 200 cycles can easily light up 28 light-emitting diodes (LEDs) and maintain them for &gt;30 minutes after charging to 3.6&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating the excellent potential of the VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 anode for utilizations of sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406547-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/93895c11-dc11-470c-beca-5154943e3def/smll202406547-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/93895c11-dc11-470c-beca-5154943e3def/smll202406547-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9f83e2f6-b29f-4f43-82fc-eb2e9b6584f7/smll202406547-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/93895c11-dc11-470c-beca-5154943e3def/smll202406547-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406547-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406547" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram of the device for NFP//VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 full cell. b) GCD curves of the anode/cathode half cells and the full cell. c) The long-term cycling performance of the NFP//VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 full cell at 0.2 C. d) Rate performance of NFP//VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 full cell at current densities 0f 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202406547-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202406547-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>Briefly, we adopted a one-step solvothermal method to prepare 3D VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs composites of VS<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles anchored to the surfaces of hollow carbon porous microspheres via C−O−V and S−O−C interfacial bonds, which reduce the bandgap and sodium ion diffusion energy barriers to increase the electrical conductivity of the materials, and also mitigate the volume swelling during sodiation/desodiation cycles, ensuring high capacity and long-term cycle stability. Consequently, the reversible capacity of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs-8 composite was 612 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and the long-term stable capacity was 498.8 and 458 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at high current densities of 5 and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 1000 cycles and 500 cycles. Moreover, in situ XRD and XPS studies performed during charge/discharge cycling revealed the reversible sodiation and desodiation process of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs. The fully assembled cell exhibits a high energy density of 257.1&nbsp;W h kg<sup>−1</sup> at a power output of 41.77&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>. In conclusion, the comprehensive characterization and electrochemical evaluation confirm the potential of VS<sub>4</sub>-HCMSs as advanced anode materials for sodium-ion batteries, offering insights into structural design and storage mechanisms, which is beneficial for promoting sodium-based energy storage devices.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202406547-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202406547-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p>All details of material synthesis, characterization, and electrochemical testing information are described in the supporting information.</p>
         </section>
